function ham_news_behavior(){
if(document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container')){
	let HAM = document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container');
if(HAM.querySelector('form')){
let HAMFORM=HAM.querySelector('form');


HAMFORM.addEventListener("submit", function(e){
    e.preventDefault();
    
    var S = document.createElement('script');
    let groups = {};
    HAM.querySelectorAll("#groups input").forEach(function(I) {
        if(I.checked) {
            groups[I.getAttribute("name")] = true;
        }
    });

    var xhr = new XMLHttpRequest();
    xhr.open('POST', 'https://www.protocol.com/core/mail_lists/mailchimp/access/');
    xhr.setRequestHeader('Content-Type', 'application/json');
    xhr.onload = function() {
        if (xhr.status === 200) {
            var response = JSON.parse(xhr.responseText);
            let resp = HAM.querySelector('#MC_resp');
            //resetBAR(resp);

            if (response.created !== true) {
              resp.classList.add('bad');
              resp.innerHTML = 'Temporary error. Please try again later.';
            } else {
              resp.classList.add('good');
              email.value = '';
              resp.innerHTML = 'Thank you for subscribing!';
            }
        } else if (xhr.status === 400) {
            var response = JSON.parse(xhr.responseText);
            let resp = HAM.querySelector('#MC_resp');
            //resetBAR(resp);
            resp.innerHTML = response.error;
            resp.classList.add('bad');
        }
    };
    xhr.send(JSON.stringify({
        email: HAM.querySelector('#MC_email').value,
        group_ids: groups,
        list_id: 'cd425f6479'
    }));
});

}}
}
  
(function(){

  if (window.innerWidth < 768){
    let menu_global=document.querySelector('.rebelbar');
      if(menu_global){if(document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container')){
	  menu_global.querySelector('.site-share-placeholder').insertAdjacentElement('afterend',document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container').cloneNode(true));}
	  if(document.querySelectorAll('.hamburger_newsletter_container')[1]){document.querySelectorAll('.hamburger_newsletter_container')[1].remove(); }
        ham_news_behavior();
      }
  }else{
    var navbarItems = document.querySelectorAll('.navbar-sections__item');

    navbarItems.forEach(function(el) {
      var dropdown = el.querySelector('.navbar-sections__dropdown')
      if(dropdown) {
        el.onmouseover = function() {
          el.classList.add('active')
          if (typeof checkRunnerLazyLoadedItems === "function") {
            checkRunnerLazyLoadedItems();
          }
        }
        el.onmouseout = function() {
          el.classList.remove('active')
        }
      }
    });
    
    let menu_global=document.querySelector('.menu-global');
    if(menu_global){
		if(document.querySelector('.site-links')){
      menu_global.insertAdjacentElement('afterbegin',document.querySelector('.site-links').cloneNode(true));
		}
	if(document.querySelector('.site-share-placeholder')){
	menu_global.querySelector('.site-links').insertAdjacentElement('afterend',document.querySelector('.site-share-placeholder').cloneNode(true));}
	  
	  if(document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container')){
      menu_global.querySelector('.site-share-placeholder').insertAdjacentElement('afterend',document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container').cloneNode(true));
	}
	  
      document.querySelectorAll('.hamburger_newsletter_container')[1].remove(); document.querySelector('.hamburger_newsletter_container').style.display="block";
      ham_news_behavior();
    }
  }

})();

</div><!-- User Code -->
<!-- End User Code --><div class="post-partial article__body" id="sPost_0_0_5_0_0_4_2_0_1_0_1_2"><span class="js-scroll-trigger" data-target="body" data-toggle-class="with-floating-shares" data-triggers="render-rebelbar"></span>
<div id="utm_campaign"></div><div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url css-listicle-body-2645216202" data-authors="Janko Roettgers" data-elid="2645216202" data-headline="Inside the collapse of Daqri's $300M bet on AR" data-post-url="https://www.protocol.com/daqri-snap-ar-failure"><div class="body-description"><p>Everything went wrong for Daqri. The startup rode a wave of augmented reality hype and about $300 million in funding to a series of half-baked products before failing spectacularly and shutting down last year. </p><p>One of Daqri's last remnants was recently acquired by Snap: The company confirmed to Protocol that late last year it took on certain Daqri assets and about two-dozen employees, who now work in the company's newly opened Vienna office under the leadership of former Daqri CTO Daniel Wagner. Snap didn't disclose the purchase price, but the timing lines up with a $34 million acquisition disclosed in its annual report to shareholders.</p><div class="horizontal-rule"></div><h4>Get what matters in tech, in your inbox every morning. <a href="http://bit.ly/2UqeOM7" target="_blank">Sign up for Source Code.</a></h4><div class="horizontal-rule"></div><p>In interviews, more than 10 former employees detailed Daqri's demise. In its decade of existence, the company built a retro-futuristic augmented reality helmet with a hefty $15,000 price tag, used fancy videos to sell the world on visual computing, and acquired a series of other startups in offices around the globe, only to see it all slip away when its glossy AR dreams collided with reality. It's a story that anyone working in the AR and VR space should take to heart, if only to avoid a similar downfall.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="1">
</div>
</div></p><h3>The early days, and a big pivot</h3><p>Daqri launched in 2010 as an early mobile AR company that used QR codes and real-world objects to create interactive experiences on smartphones. Its first projects included <a href="https://www.amazon.com/DAQRI-Crayola-Color-Alive/dp/B00SID3R56/ref=sr_1_2?qid=1582062296&amp;refinements=p_4%3ADAQRI&amp;s=mobile-apps&amp;sr=1-2" target="_blank">a multiyear partnership with Crayola</a>, which used Daqri's technology to turn coloring book pages into digital 3D objects on your phone.</p><p>The company also worked on a platform to let others develop their own AR content and experiences, but executives quickly realized that they were way too early for consumer AR. In 2014, a Kickstarter campaign for Daqri-developed interactive blocks <a href="https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/daqri/elements-4d-interactive-blocks/posts" target="_blank">attracted fewer than 200 backers</a>, which was enough to fund the project but hardly a critical mass.</p><p>That's when company executives decided to change course and focus on businesses instead of consumers. It's also when Daqri started to get massive cash infusions from <a href="https://tarsadia.com" target="_blank">Tarsadia Investments</a>, a Southern California-based private equity company. And, according to a number of its former employees, it's when a lot of Daqri's troubles started.</p><p>Former employees who talked to Protocol all did so on the condition of anonymity for fear of retaliation, including from the company's main funder, which still controls legal entities connected to Daqri. Some also expressed concerns about getting singled out at their current place of work for talking to the media.</p><p>As one might expect, any company that ends up laying off most of its employees will leave some with ill feelings. It's also worth noting that not everyone who Protocol talked with was critical of Daqri. Depending on their time of employment, position and location, some praised Daqri, its leadership, and job perks that included unlimited vacation, yoga classes and nap rooms, as well as frequent offsite events and parties. </p><p>Daqri's founding CEO Brian Mullins, his successor Roy Ashok and its main investor Tarsadia did not reply to requests for comment.</p><h3>From a $5 million valuation to $275M in funding, with strings attached</h3><p>There aren't a lot of public disclosures about Daqri's funding, but <a href="https://trellis.law/ruling/SC120777/1639-11th-streetinc-vs-philip-tolk-et-al/2019030581c1df" target="_blank">a lawsuit of a co-founder,</a> who alleged that he got cheated out of his fair share, reveals that the company was valued at $5 million in an asset transfer in May 2013. The company <a href="https://www.wired.com/2013/06/augmented-reality-daqri/" target="_blank">announced a $15 million fundraise</a> later that year, and Tarsadia went on to prop up Daqri with a steady flow of cash that had amounted to <a href="https://labusinessjournal.com/news/2017/oct/13/daqri-ceo-mullins-exits-ar-developer/" target="_blank">a reported $275 million</a> by 2017. Sources told Protocol the total amount put into the company was in the $300 million range.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="2">
</div>
</div></p><p>Tarsadia had made several bets in the pharmaceutical space and saw in Daqri a chance to invest early in a nascent technology that could be transformative to the workplace. Daqri employees with knowledge of the relationship told Protocol that Tarsadia was anything but a typical startup investor. Instead of taking a minority stake, the private equity company bought out Daqri. "None of the employees really owned any part of the company," said a long-time senior employee.</p><p>The ownership structure was problematic, multiple former employees told Protocol. "The incentives were wrong," said one former employee. "Everyone had already cashed out." </p><p>Daqri's executives were left without power, effectively turning them into subordinates. "It was a group of middle management that was reporting to the investors," the former senior employee said. Tarsadia executives are even said to have taken part in Daqri's executive off-sites, which resulted in Daqri's leadership avoiding frank discussions about problems at the company. "The investor was always in the room," the former senior employee said.</p><h3>A futuristic helmet that remained a prototype</h3><p>As part of its focus on businesses, Daqri <a href="https://www.businesswire.com/news/home/20140908006433/en/DAQRI-Unveils-4D-Smart-Helmet-Industrial-Workforce" target="_blank">announced the development</a> of a futuristic-looking hard hat with integrated AR glasses in 2014. It was an audacious project that was pitched to construction companies, manufacturers of heavy machinery, and even the U.S. Navy. </p><p>The project required a lot of technical expertise so Daqri embarked on a spending spree, acquiring <a href="https://techcrunch.com/2015/05/13/daqri-acquires-ar-pioneer-artoolworks/" target="_blank">AR software specialist ARToolworks</a>, <a href="https://www.mobihealthnews.com/40697/daqri-buys-eeg-tracker-melon-to-build-out-augmented-reality-helmet/" target="_blank">EEG headband maker Melon</a>, <a href="https://techcrunch.com/2016/03/01/daqri-acquires-1066-labs-to-power-its-augmented-reality-smart-helmet/" target="_blank">manufacturing startup 1066 Labs</a> and <a href="https://www.forbes.com/sites/aarontilley/2016/03/28/augmented-reality-startup-daqri-acquires-hologram-maker-to-take-on-microsoft-hololens/#59a6fccf3e5c" target="_blank">holographic display developer Two Trees Photonics</a>. The company quickly ballooned to around 400 employees from different corporate cultures in six offices in Los Angeles, the Bay Area, Ireland, the U.K. and Austria. </p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="3">
</div>
</div></p><p>And the technical challenges remained immense. Daqri initially bet on Android to power the helmet, only to change course and switch to Linux soon before the product was supposed to ship in 2016. "A lot of people were unhappy" about that switch, said one employee who worked on the project for multiple years.</p><p>Daqri also learned the hard way that you can't develop workplace products exclusively in the lab. The helmet, which cost $15,000, was a highly specialized piece of equipment with sensors like a thermal camera that made for a good sales pitch. Workers in the field on the other hand often cared about much simpler features, like the ability to see what's behind them. Or they didn't want to be bothered with technology at all and found all the bells and whistles alienating. "We received pushback for the same exact thing that gave us orders," said the former employee who had been working on the project. </p><p>On top of that, Daqri had trouble obtaining the necessary certifications needed to use the helmet in industrial workplaces. "It was a product that was never finished for scale deployment," said the former senior employee. "I wouldn't say that it was functional," said the former employee who worked on the project.</p><p>Another employee, who was with the company about four years, had an even franker assessment: "None of it was real."</p><h3>Marketing as a core part of the business</h3><p>You wouldn't have known about any of the issues from looking at Daqri's marketing. The company received <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=wTp82DbJ-1o" target="_blank">favorable coverage</a> from <a href="https://blogs.wsj.com/digits/2014/09/05/augmented-reality-experts-unveil-hardhat-2-0/" target="_blank">a number of</a> <a href="https://techcrunch.com/video/daqris-smart-helmet-hands-on/" target="_blank">publications</a>. It also churned out a steady stream of polished videos on its own, showing Daqri's helmet <a href="https://vimeo.com/104034319" target="_blank">in a variety of industrial settings</a>, on <a href="https://vimeo.com/247194545" target="_blank">a Navy ship</a>, and, inexplicably, worn by a man <a href="https://vimeo.com/250506584" target="_blank">donning a jet suit, Iron Man-style.</a></p><p>"There were people making videos all the time," said the former senior employee. "It absolutely was showmanship. That was part of the company DNA." </p><p>"That became a core part of the business, that marketing arm," said another employee.</p><p>In conversations with Protocol, multiple employees attributed some of Daqri's heavy focus on marketing and promotion to its proximity to Hollywood. The company's Los Angeles headquarters was in the Los Angeles Center Studios facility, where it occupied two floors, including one that used to <a href="https://www.businessinsider.com/what-is-the-daqri-smart-helmet-2016-4" target="_blank">house the set for AMC's "Mad Men"</a> and where decorations included movie props. </p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="4">
</div>
</div></p><p>Daqri's relentless marketing wasn't just about selling AR to the world. It was also meant to woo the investor-owner, and ultimately convince staffers of the company's potential as well. In one instance, Daqri staff even produced a video for an internal holiday party that portrayed senior executives in the future, talking about how big of a success the company had become.</p><p>It worked: While some were skeptical about aspects of Daqri's technology, many bought into the vision of its founders. "We didn't even see the demise coming," said one former employee.</p><h3>Layoffs, spin-offs, 'Hunger Games' jokes</h3><p>That demise started around the beginning of 2017, when it became increasingly clear that Daqri's smart helmet wasn't going anywhere. In March 2017, Business Insider <a href="https://www.businessinsider.com/vr-company-daqri-is-laying-off-staff-worldwide-2017-2" target="_blank">reported that 80 employees</a> worldwide would be laid off. It resulted in the closing of two offices, according to a former employee.</p><p>Additional layoffs followed every couple of months. In Los Angeles, one of the two office floors quickly emptied and was subleased to another company. Former employees said that the atmosphere at the company quickly changed, with some criticizing the executive leadership for not handling the layoffs well.</p><p>Staffers who were let go were forced to leave the office as quickly as possible, at times without having a chance to say their goodbyes. One employee who stayed on well into 2018 said that it had turned into the most toxic workplace of their career. Another employee recalled that the team started to make "Hunger Games" jokes. "It was very stressful," that employee said. "The company was just hemorrhaging people."</p><p>At about the same time, the company shifted its efforts from the smart helmet to a simpler set of AR glasses that retailed for $5,000 — more than Microsoft's HoloLens AR headset, which first went on sale for $3,000 in 2016. Daqri's leadership was swapped out, with Chief Product Officer Roy Ashok taking over as CEO, and founding CEO Brian Mullins being forced to leave.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="5">
</div>
</div></p><p>By this point, investor-owner Tarsadia was already looking for ways to minimize its losses. Instead of pouring in more money, Tarsadia proceeded to spin off some of the company's acquisitions into separate entities. The Two Tree Photonics team, which had started to develop AR for car windshields while at Daqri, incorporated as <a href="https://envisics.com/" target="_blank">Envisics</a>. A team headed by early Xbox architect Seamus Blackley, who <a href="https://venturebeat.com/2017/03/15/xbox-cofounder-seamus-blackley-takes-rd-post-at-augmented-reality-firm-daqri-2/" target="_blank">had joined Daqri in March 2017</a>, got spun off into an R&amp;D company called <a href="https://www.pacificlightandhologram.com/" target="_blank">Pacific Light &amp; Hologram</a>.</p><p>Daqri's staff dwindled in subsequent months, and the company ultimately shut down operations and let go its final staff <a href="https://techcrunch.com/2019/09/13/daily-crunch-ar-startup-daqri-is-shutting-down/" target="_blank">in September 2019</a>. Its office furniture was auctioned off <a href="https://industrialbid.com/m/view-auctions/catalog/id/4800" target="_blank">soon after</a>, and its patent portfolio <a href="https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/the-daqri-augmented-reality-patent-portfolio-offered-for-sale-or-license-300999176.html" target="_blank">went up for sale earlier this month</a>. The lawsuit of Daqri's co-founder against the company is still ongoing and is expected to go before a jury soon.</p><h3>Lessons from Daqri's failure</h3><p>Asked why Daqri failed, former employees offered a range of opinions. Many of them cited excessive spending fueled by big checks, a lack of direction, and the inability to pivot from projects in time. They also cited the role of the owner-investor and failures of leadership.</p><p>But ultimately, the demise also has a lot to do with factors that aren't all that unique to Daqri: Making hardware is difficult, as is inventing products for a use case nobody yet wants. That's a problem that AR companies like Magic Leap face as well. The Florida-based startup, which raised over $2.6 billion in funding to date and <a href="https://variety.com/2019/digital/news/magic-leap-series-e-funding-1203401035/" target="_blank">is currently looking for more</a>, is also known for blue-sky marketing that aims to not just sell its product, but AR in general. </p><p>Like Daqri, Magic Leap and Microsoft have also more recently pegged their hopes for AR's commercial success to enterprise use cases, betting that companies will buy hardware at price points out of reach to the average consumer. But as Daqri shows, selling to big companies, and getting AR to work in industrial contexts, can be extremely challenging as well.</p><p>And while Daqri may have failed, it may still play a significant part in the future of AR. "Daqri was also the beginning of a lot of people's careers," said an employee who went on to work for a different AR company. If anything, alumni could help other companies avoid the same mistakes.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="6">
</div>
</div></p><p>Lessons learned from Daqri's failure may be valuable to any company looking to build AR products, including Snap. The Vienna team formed from Daqi's wreckage is said to be working on AR software for the company.</p><p>Software, one might add, that could ultimately also power a future AR version of the company's Spectacles wearable — just don't expect it to look like a helmet.</p></div>
</div>

<script>
        (function() {
            if (! window.Blaize) {
                window.Blaize = {
                    formPreProcessors: {},
                    formPostProcessors: {},
                    registerFormPreProcessor: function(formSlug, processor) {
                        if (! window.Blaize.formPreProcessors[formSlug]) window.Blaize.formPreProcessors[formSlug] = [];

                        if (typeof processor === "function") {
                            window.Blaize.formPreProcessors[formSlug].push(processor);
                        }
                    },
                    registerFormPostProcessor: function(formSlug, processor) {
                        if (! window.Blaize.formPostProcessors[formSlug]) window.Blaize.formPostProcessors[formSlug] = [];

                        if (typeof processor === "function") {
                            window.Blaize.formPostProcessors[formSlug].push(processor);
                        }
                    },
                    preProcessFormData: function(formSlug, formData) {
                        if (window.Blaize.formPreProcessors[formSlug] || window.Blaize.formPreProcessors['*']) {
                            var processors = [];
                            if (window.Blaize.formPreProcessors['*']) {
                                window.Blaize.formPreProcessors['*'].forEach(function(processor) {
                                    processors.push(processor);
                                });
                            }
                            if (window.Blaize.formPreProcessors[formSlug]) {
                                window.Blaize.formPreProcessors[formSlug].forEach(function(processor) {
                                    processors.push(processor);
                                });
                            }
                            processors.forEach(function(processor) {
                                processedFormData = processor(formData);
                                if (processedFormData != undefined) {
                                    formData = processedFormData;
                                }
                            });
                        }

                        return formData;
                    },
                    postProcessFormData: function(formSlug, formData, response) {
                        if (window.Blaize.formPostProcessors[formSlug] || window.Blaize.formPostProcessors['*']) {
                            var processors = [];
                            if (window.Blaize.formPostProcessors['*']) {
                                window.Blaize.formPostProcessors['*'].forEach(function(processor) {
                                    processors.push(processor);
                                });
                            }
                            if (window.Blaize.formPostProcessors[formSlug]) {
                                window.Blaize.formPostProcessors[formSlug].forEach(function(processor) {
                                    processors.push(processor);
                                });
                            }
                            processors.forEach(function(processor) {
                                processor(formData, response);
                            });
                        }
                    }
                }
            }

            var section = document.getElementById("form-500d3dc9-2165-4921-b1e0-48028c47607f");
            var formRegister = section.querySelector('.blaize-form-register');
            var formLogin = section.querySelector('.blaize-form-login');
            var registerSsiError = formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-ssi-error');
            var registerError = formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-error');
            var registerSuccess = formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-success');
            var loginError = formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-error');
            var loginSuccess = formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-success');
            var identifiersFieldset = formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-identifiers');
            var validatorsFieldset = formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-validators');

            section.querySelector('.blaize-to-login a').onclick = function(event) {
                event.preventDefault();
                formRegister.style.display = 'none';
                formLogin.style.display = 'block';
            };

            section.querySelector('.blaize-to-register a').onclick = function(event) {
                event.preventDefault();
                formLogin.style.display = 'none';
                formRegister.style.display = 'block';
            };


            var blaizeRequest = function(url, action, data, callback) {
                var xhr = new(XMLHttpRequest || ActiveXObject)('MSXML2.XMLHTTP.3.0');
                xhr.open('POST', url, true);
                xhr.setRequestHeader('Content-type', 'application/json');
                xhr.setRequestHeader('Accept', 'application/json');

                xhr.onreadystatechange = function() {
                    if (xhr.readyState === 4) {
                        var response;
                        try {
                            response = JSON.parse(xhr.response);
                        } catch (e) {
                            response = xhr.response;
                        }

                        return callback(xhr.status, response);
                    }
                };

                return (!data) ? xhr.send() : xhr.send(data);
            };

            var handleRegister = function(event) {
                event.preventDefault();
                registerError.style.display = 'none';
                    var identifier = registrationAuthenticationIdentifier ? registrationAuthenticationIdentifier : formRegister.querySelector('input[name="email_address"]').value;
                    var identifierKey = "email_address";

                var attributes = {};
                var inputs = formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-user-attributes').querySelectorAll('input, textarea, select');
                for (var i = 0; i < inputs.length; i++) {
                    if (inputs[i].type === 'checkbox') {
                        attributes[inputs[i].name] = inputs[i].checked;
                    } else if (inputs[i].type === 'radio') {
                        if (inputs[i].checked === true) {
                            attributes[inputs[i].name] = inputs[i].value;
                        }
                    } else {
                        attributes[inputs[i].name] = inputs[i].value;
                    }
                }

                var registerData = {
                    identifiers: {},
                    validators: {},
                    attributes: attributes
                };


                if (registrationAuthenticationToken) {
                    registerData.validators["token_exchange"] = registrationAuthenticationToken;
                } else {
                    registerData.identifiers[identifierKey] = identifier;

                    var activeElementAction = document.activeElement.getAttribute("formaction");
                    if (activeElementAction === "password") {
                        registerData.validators["password"] = formRegister.querySelector('input[name="password"]').value;
                    }
                }

                registerData = window.Blaize.preProcessFormData(formRegister.getAttribute('data-blaize-form'), registerData);

                blaizeRequest('/blaize/register', 'register', JSON.stringify(registerData), function(status, response) {
                    window.Blaize.postProcessFormData(formRegister.getAttribute('data-blaize-form'), registerData, response);

                    if (status === 200) {
                            if (window.zephrSocialSigninRegistration) {
                                location.reload(true);
                                return;
                            }
                            var passwordlessData = {
                                identifiers: {
                                    email_address: identifier
                                },
                                delivery: {
                                    method: 'email',
                                    destination: identifier,
                                    action: 'register',
                                    redirect: window.location.href.replace(window.location.origin, "")
                                }
                            };

                            blaizeRequest('/blaize/token-exchange', 'verify_email', JSON.stringify(passwordlessData), function(status, response) {
                                if (status === 201) {
                                    if (response.message) {
                                        if (event.target.className.indexOf("complete-registration") > -1) {
                                            formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-complete-registration').style.display = 'none';
                                        }
                                        formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-user-attributes').style.display = 'none';
                                        formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-validators').style.display = 'none';
                                        formRegister.querySelector('.blaize-to-login').style.display = 'none';

                                        registerSuccess.innerHTML = response.message;
                                        registerSuccess.style.display = 'block';
                                        setTimeout(function(){
                                        document.getElementById('popup').style.display = "none";
                                      },5000);

                                    }
                                } else {
                                    registerError.innerHTML = 'Something went wrong! Please try again later.';
                                    registerError.style.display = 'block';
                                }
                            });
                    } else if (status === 409) {
                        if (identifierKey === 'username') {
                            registerError.innerHTML = 'Username already taken, please try a different one.';
                        } else {
                            registerError.innerHTML = 'This email address is already associated with an account. Please log in or enter a different email address.';
                        }
                        registerError.style.display = 'block';
                    } else if (status === 400 || status === 402) {
                        registerError.innerHTML = response.message;
                        registerError.style.display = 'block';
                    } else {
                        registerError.innerHTML = 'Something went wrong! Please try again later.';
                        registerError.style.display = 'block';
                    }
                });
            };

            var handleLogin = function(event) {
                event.preventDefault();
                loginError.style.display = 'none';

                    var identifier = formLogin.querySelector('input[name="email_address"]').value;
                    var isAUserName = false;

                var endpoint;
                var payloadData;

                if (event.target.className.indexOf("passwordless") > -1) {
                    endpoint = '/blaize/token-exchange';
                    payloadData = JSON.stringify({
                        identifiers: {
                            email_address: identifier
                        },
                        delivery: {
                            method: 'email',
                            destination: identifier,
                            action: 'login',
                            redirect: window.location.href.replace(window.location.origin, "")
                        }
                    });
                } else {
                    endpoint = '/blaize/login';


                        if (isAUserName) {
                            payloadData = JSON.stringify({
                                identifiers: {
                                    username: identifier
                                },
                                validators: {
                                    password: formLogin.querySelector('input[name="password"]').value
                                }
                            });
                        } else {
                            payloadData = JSON.stringify({
                                identifiers: {
                                    email_address: identifier
                                },
                                validators: {
                                    password: formLogin.querySelector('input[name="password"]').value
                                }
                            });
                        }
                }

                blaizeRequest(endpoint, 'login', payloadData, function(status, response) {
                    if (status === 200) {
                        if (response.cookie) {
                            document.cookie = response.cookie;
                            location.reload(true);
                        }
                    } else if (status === 201) {
                        if (response.message) {
                            loginSuccess.innerHTML = response.message;
                            loginSuccess.style.display = 'block';
                            if (event.target.className.indexOf("passwordless") > -1) {
                                formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-login-passwordless').style.display = 'none';
                            } else {
                                formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-submit').style.display = 'none';
                            }
                            formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-validators').style.display = 'none';
                            formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-to-register').style.display = 'none';
                            setTimeout(function(){
                            document.getElementById('popup').style.display = "none";
                          },5000);
                        }
                    } else if (status === 401) {
                        if (isAUserName) {
                            loginError.innerHTML = 'The username or password you entered are incorrect.';
                        } else {
                            loginError.innerHTML = 'The email address or password you entered are incorrect.';
                        }
                        loginError.style.display = 'block';
                    } else if (status === 404) {
                        loginError.innerHTML = 'User not found.';
        loginError.style.display = 'block';
      } else if (status === 429) {
          loginError.innerHTML = 'To gain login access, please contact your administrator.';
          loginError.style.display = 'block';
      } else {
        loginError.innerHTML = 'Something went wrong! Please try again later.';
        loginError.style.display = 'block';
      }
    });
  };


            var registrationAuthenticationToken;
            var registrationAuthenticationIdentifier;
            var loginAuthenticationToken;


            formRegister.addEventListener('submit', handleRegister);

            formLogin.addEventListener('submit', handleLogin);
                formLogin.querySelector('.blaize-login-passwordless').addEventListener('click', handleLogin);

        })();
    </script>

        document.addEventListener(
        "click",
        function(event) {
          // If user either clicks X button OR clicks outside the modal window, then close modal by calling closeModal()
          if (
            event.target.matches(".close") ||
            !event.target.closest("#popup")
          ) {
            closePopup()
          }
        },
        false
        )

        function closePopup() {
          document.querySelector("#popup").style.display = "none"
        }

    </script>

<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="Meta offers a glimpse at next-generation VR headsets" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.protocol.com/entertainment/meta-vr-headsets-prototypes">
        Meta offers a glimpse at next-generation VR headsets
    </a>
</h2>
<div class="widget__subheadline">
<h2 class="widget__subheadline-text h2" data-type="text"><p>Meta previewed some of its experimental VR prototypes. The company says these headsets will pave the way for next-generation devices. </p></h2>
</div></div><div class="widget__head"><a aria-label="Meta offers a glimpse at next-generation VR headsets" href="https://www.protocol.com/entertainment/meta-vr-headsets-prototypes"><div aria-label="Mark Zuckerberg wears VR headset" class="widget__image crop-16x9" data-runner-img-hd="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/mark-zuckerberg-wears-vr-headset.jpg?id=29991272&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-md="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/mark-zuckerberg-wears-vr-headset.jpg?id=29991272&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-sd="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/mark-zuckerberg-wears-vr-headset.jpg?id=29991272&amp;width=980&amp;quality=85" data-runner-style="padding-bottom: 56.25%;" data-type="image" role="img" style='background-image: url("https://www.protocol.com/media-library/mark-zuckerberg-wears-vr-headset.jpg?id=29991272&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700"); padding-bottom: 56.25%;'><div class="lazyload-placeholder">

<a class="social-author__name" data-type="text" href="https://www.protocol.com/u/nick">Nick Statt</a>
<div class="social-author__bio"><p>
	Nick Statt is Protocol's video game reporter. Prior to joining Protocol, he was news editor at The Verge covering the gaming industry, mobile apps and antitrust out of San Francisco, in addition to managing coverage of Silicon Valley tech giants and startups. He now resides in Rochester, New York, home of the garbage plate and, completely coincidentally, the World Video Game Hall of Fame. He can be reached at nstatt@protocol.com.
</p></div>
</div><div class="social-date">
<span class="social-date__text">June 20, 2022</span>
</div><div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url css-listicle-body-2657526898" data-authors="Nick Statt" data-elid="2657526898" data-headline="Meta offers a glimpse at next-generation VR headsets" data-post-url="https://www.protocol.com/entertainment/meta-vr-headsets-prototypes"><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>Meta is pulling back the curtain on some of its experimental virtual-reality projects, showcasing a number of new prototypes the company says will help it advance display technology to the point of being indistinguishable from real life. </p><p>In a press briefing last week, Mark Zuckerberg and Meta Reality Labs Chief Scientist Michael Abrash discussed a handful of VR devices, each designed to improve an element of VR. They all have colorful names, too, like Butterscotch, Starburst, Holocake and Mirror Lake. </p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template><p>The goal, Zuckerberg says, is for headsets of the future to pass a so-called “visual Turing test,” referencing the imitation game designed to test the sophistication of artificial intelligence. In this context, however, Meta wants its VR headset to display imagery that passes for the real world, a feat it considers “the holy grail of display research.” </p><p>“Today’s VR headsets deliver incredible 3D visual experiences, but the experience still differs in many ways from what we see in the real world. They have a lower resolution than what’s offered by laptops, TVs and phones; the lenses distort the wearer’s view; and they cannot be used for extended periods of time,” the company explained. “To get there, we need to build an unprecedented type of VR display system — a lightweight display that is so advanced it can deliver what our eyes need to function naturally so they perceive we are looking at the real world in VR.”</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="1">
</div>
</div></p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image">
<img class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="ed9aff45c7bd03557f1f75d4b2483266" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image" id="c4fdc" loading="lazy" src="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/meta-s-holocake-2-combines-a-u00a0holographic-lens-with-a-so-called-polarization-based-optical-folding-technique-to-drastically-bring-down-the-weight-and-size-of-the-device.jpg?id=29991459&amp;width=980"/>
<small class="image-media media-caption" placeholder="Add Photo Caption...">Meta's Holocake 2 combines a\u00a0holographic lens with a so-called polarization-based optical folding technique to drastically bring down the weight and size of the device.</small>
<small class="image-media media-photo-credit" placeholder="Add Photo Credit...">
            Image: Meta
        </small>
</p><p>Meta has reoriented its entire social networking business around a mission to build the metaverse, with Zuckerberg calling it the next big leap for the internet after mobile and a cornerstone of how we might socialize, play games, shop, work and communicate in the future. But a key element of Meta’s specific <a href="https://www.protocol.com/entertainment/mark-zuckerberg-interview-metaverse-vr" target="_self">vision</a> for the metaverse involves hardware: in particular, VR headsets, augmented-reality glasses and mixed-reality devices that might be able to combine the two technologies and push them into the mainstream. </p><p>A key early advantage for Meta is its investments in such hardware, like its 2014 acquisition of Oculus that helped it gain a <a href="https://www.investors.com/news/technology/virtual-reality-meta-dominates-a-small-market/#:~:text=Meta%20Dominates%20Virtual%20Reality%20Headsets,with%20Meta%20taking%2062%25%20share." rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">massive 80% market share</a> in VR hardware sales. But VR adoption, while growing fast during the pandemic, lags miles behind other consumer electronics categories; AR, outside of smartphone apps, mobile games and some select glasses brands, has yet to materialize in any meaningful fashion.</p><p>So Meta is now going to great lengths, and spending billions, to ensure it can maintain its edge on AR and VR before competitors like Apple, Microsoft and others begin releasing consumer devices that can compete with Meta’s wireless Quest headset, its <a href="https://www.protocol.com/entertainment/meta-quest-presence-platform-zuckerberg" target="_self">forthcoming Project Cambria headset</a> and <a href="https://www.protocol.com/bulletins/meta-portal-pivot" target="_self">its planned AR devices</a>. Though Meta is reportedly <a href="https://www.theinformation.com/articles/meta-scales-back-ar-glasses-plan-amid-reality-labs-shakeup" target="_blank">cutting back</a> on Reality Labs spending of late after incurring $10 billion in losses last year, the company is intent on showing how it’s still pushing the state of the art forward.<br/></p><p>For Meta, that means continuing to try to bring down the size and weight of devices, increase resolution and making VR more comfortable. The prototypes it showed last week each target a different hurdle VR faces today. For instance, the Half Dome series of prototypes, which Meta began building back in 2017, experiments with varifocal technology that “ensures the focus is correct and enables clear and comfortable vision within arm’s length for extended periods of time,” while also bringing down size and weight so headsets are less taxing on the head and neck.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="2">
</div>
</div></p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image">
<img alt="Meta's Half Dome prototype series. " class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="4bf22500fdd52139e0dec5187a76cd1a" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image" id="b3d18" loading="lazy" src="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/meta-s-half-dome-prototype-series.jpg?id=29991491&amp;width=980"/>
<small class="image-media media-caption" placeholder="Add Photo Caption...">The Half Dome series of prototypes are designed to experiment with varifocal technology and in slimming down VR headset form factors. </small><small class="image-media media-photo-credit" placeholder="Add Photo Credit...">Image: Meta</small></p><p>The Butterscotch prototype aims to produce “retinal” resolution, or image quality similar to that of the human eye. Meta said it shrunk down the field of view of a standard Quest 2 headset and replaced the lens with something higher quality, allowing for much higher resolution, though in a package that was “nowhere near shippable” due to its bulk, Meta said.</p><p>Starburst, on the other hand, is an HDR VR headset — one of the first of its kind, the company claims — that can reproduce levels of brightness seen by the human eye in an indoor or nighttime scenario. But it’s also far too large, requiring a cable tethering it to an external processing device and a form factor that requires users hold it like a pair of binoculars.</p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image">
<img alt="Meta's Starburst prototype headset. " class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="73c837ec2f32e107fd67e0da7120255b" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image" id="758ee" loading="lazy" src="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/meta-s-starburst-prototype-headset.jpg?id=29991537&amp;width=980"/>
<small class="image-media media-caption" placeholder="Add Photo Caption...">Meta's Starburst is one of the first of its kind HDR VR headsets, the company claims, allowing for more realistic depictions of brightness. </small><small class="image-media media-photo-credit" placeholder="Add Photo Credit...">Image: Meta</small></p><p>The final two prototypes Meta unveiled are Holocake 2 and Mirror Lake. The former is the thinnest and lightest VR headset the company has ever made. It resembles a pair of  sunglasses, but is capable of playing VR games on PC when tethered thanks to its holographic optics and its so-called polarization-based optical folding, or “pancake” optics, a technique that effectively squishes the display panel and lens closer together. The Holocake 2 builds on Meta's previous work in holographic optics, which it <a href="https://research.facebook.com/blog/2020/06/holographic-optics-for-thin-and-lightweight-virtual-reality/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">detailed back in 2020</a>.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="3">
</div>
</div></p><p>Meta says Mirror Lake is not so much a prototype as it is a concept design that will, in time, bring together all of this technology into a single device with a lightweight form factor that shows “what a complete, next-gen display system could look like.” That said, it may be many years before Mirror Lake materializes as a wearable device, and potentially many more before a consumer device is able to combine all of these advancements together in one package and pass Meta’s visual Turing test.</p></template></div>

</div><div class="social-author clearfix">
<div class="social-author__avatar image">
<div class="cover-all image" style='background-image: url("https://www.protocol.com/media-library/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNDc3NDkwMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTcxNjM0MDk0N30.TXKgQqnOmZMAdim6PhxXsqUkcJo_7gKhii0Tk41D6tQ/image.jpg?width=210")'></div>

<a class="social-author__name" data-type="text" href="https://www.protocol.com/u/jamesdaly">James Daly</a>
<div class="social-author__bio">James Daly has a deep knowledge of creating brand voice identity, including understanding various audiences and targeting messaging accordingly. He enjoys commissioning, editing, writing, and business development, particularly in launching new ventures and building passionate audiences. Daly has led teams large and small to multiple awards and quantifiable success through a strategy built on teamwork, passion, fact-checking, intelligence, analytics, and audience growth while meeting budget goals and production deadlines in fast-paced environments. Daly is the Editorial Director of 2030 Media and a contributor at Wired. </div>
</div><div class="social-date">
<span class="social-date__text">June 14, 2022</span>
</div><div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url css-listicle-body-2657454032" data-authors="James Daly" data-elid="2657454032" data-headline="Chips are the backbone of our digital economy. We must rebuild the American chip industry or suffer the consequences" data-post-url="https://www.protocol.com/sponsored-content/chips-are-the-backbone-of-our-digital-economy-we-must-rebuild-the-american-chip-industry-or-suffer-the-consequences"><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>Some of the most astounding tech-enabled advances of the next decade, from cutting-edge medical research to urban traffic control and factory floor optimization, will be enabled by a device often smaller than a thumbnail: the memory chip. </p><p>While vast amounts of data are created, stored and processed every moment — by some estimates,<a href="http://pubads.g.doubleclick.net/gampad/clk?id=6036474854&amp;iu=/21901267728" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow" target="_blank"> 2.5 quintillion bytes</a> daily — the insights in that code are unlocked by the memory chips that hold it and transfer it. “Memory will propel the next 10 years into the most transformative years in human history,” said Sanjay Mehrotra, president and CEO of Micron Technology. </p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template><p>A kind of semiconductor, memory chips are integrated circuits made of millions of capacitors and transistors that can store data. They are the backbone of the broader digital economy — enabling everything from safer transportation to greater broadband access and a more efficient energy grid — and they play an active part in almost every aspect of our daily life. Consider automobiles, for example, “Not long ago, automobiles contained very little memory to process data,” Mehrotra said. “Today, automobiles process huge quantities of fast-moving data in support of advanced safety features.” Electric vehicles, in essence, can be likened to data centers on wheels.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="1">
</div>
</div></p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image">
<img class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="bf33c02d65d5d3446d16da69bd80aaf7" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image" id="4c8b2" loading="lazy" src="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/image.png?id=29955508&amp;width=980"/>
</p><p><strong>The quantity of data has broader implications </strong></p><p><strong></strong>The 21st century runs on chips, but right now they can't be supported. A global shortage of semiconductors, which crimped the production of everything from pickup trucks to PlayStations, has industry experts and policymakers looking for a new strategy to avoid debilitating bottlenecks in the future supply chain of microprocessors.</p><p>Semiconductors are in short supply because of several colliding factors, including spikes in demand for consumer electronic products and concurrent slowdowns in chip production caused by the pandemic.<a href="http://pubads.g.doubleclick.net/gampad/clk?id=6035702866&amp;iu=/21901267728" target="_blank"> In 1990, the U.S. produced 37% of the world’s semiconductors. Today, it produces 12%</a>. Meanwhile, 75% of the world’s chip manufacturing is concentrated in East Asia. Micron is the only memory manufacturer remaining in the Western Hemisphere, and it makes 2% of the global memory chips in the U.S.</p><p>The increasing performance demands caused by technological leaps in artificial intelligence, 5G, the industrial Internet of Things, edge computing, autonomous cars and high-performance data centers have increased demand for chips. Every major industrial company is trying to figure out how it can manage the amount of data it generates to make its business stronger. Chips are an essential part of that. </p><p>Meanwhile, foreign countries see the strategic importance of semiconductors and use government incentives to advance manufacturing while the United States government has watched it happen while sitting on the sidelines. </p><p>Take China, which is projected to have the world’s largest share of chip production by 2030 due to an estimated $100 billion in government subsidies. How much has the United States invested in essential strategic manufacturing operations like semiconductors? Zero. And, without government incentives, U.S. domestic chip production will continue to collapse. </p><p>After years of inaction, U.S. elected leaders have taken note, and are working to implement change. The House and Senate are working on legislation designed to increase global competitiveness, including the <a href="http://pubads.g.doubleclick.net/gampad/clk?id=6034343949&amp;iu=/21901267728" target="_blank">Facilitating American-Built Semiconductors Act</a>, which aims to provide firms a 25% tax credit toward the construction, expansion or modernization of semiconductor fabrication plants and processing equipment in the U.S. </p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="2">
</div>
</div></p><p>In January 2021, Congress passed the Chips for America Act. This bipartisan legislation authorized a series of programs to promote the research, development and fabrication of semiconductors within the United States. Together, the Fabs and Chip Acts are meant to renew American competitiveness in chip manufacturing, processing, packaging and design. </p><p><span></span>Large-scale investments like those the semiconductor industry are poised to make, coupled with targeted government incentives, can meaningfully benefit the U.S. economy in both the short and long term by boosting domestic manufacturing, enhancing supply chain resiliency and increasing national security. “Government support of these capital-intensive, long-term investments serve as a multiplier for advanced manufacturing across the industry, positioning the U.S. as a central figure in the fourth Industrial Revolution,” Mehrotra said. An investment in semiconductors is an investment in the foundational technology that will support innovation across sectors and industries in the United States.</p><p><span></span>Already there are hopeful signs. <a href="http://pubads.g.doubleclick.net/gampad/clk?id=6035706865&amp;iu=/21901267728" target="_blank">A billion-dollar chip factory just opened</a> in upstate New York, and in October 2020, the Department of Defense awarded more than $197 million to help the American microelectronics companies create state-of-the-art design and manufacturing facilities. Advanced computer chips not only drive economic and scientific advancement but military capabilities. Through the Rapid Assured Microelectronics Prototypes plans, the DoD hopes to create a reliable and resilient domestic source of chips for its artificial intelligence, 5G communications, quantum computing and autonomous vehicle needs. “The microelectronics industry is at the root of our nation’s economic strength, national security and technological standing,” said Michael Kratsios, former acting undersecretary of Defense for Research and Engineering.</p><p>Ultimately, the result of the silicon resurgence — in processing power and speed as well as energy efficiency — will be to help build bold new business models, many of which we have yet to dream up. But, with the right investments in our chip-driven future, these dreams can turn into an exciting new reality and protect the future of America. </p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="3">
</div>
</div></p></template></div>

</div></div>

<a class="widget__section js-load-brick-on-click" href="https://www.protocol.com/policy/">Policy</a>
<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="The privacy debate could get 'dangerous' for the FTC" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.protocol.com/policy/ohlhausen-privacy-ftc">
        The privacy debate could get 'dangerous' for the FTC
    </a>
</h2>
<div class="widget__subheadline">
<h2 class="widget__subheadline-text h2" data-type="text"><p>Former FTC acting chair Maureen Ohlhausen has a warning for Lina Khan.</p></h2>
</div></div><div class="widget__head"><a aria-label="The privacy debate could get 'dangerous' for the FTC" href="https://www.protocol.com/policy/ohlhausen-privacy-ftc"><div aria-label="Maureen Ohlhausen" class="widget__image crop-16x9" data-runner-img-hd="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/maureen-ohlhausen.jpg?id=29989904&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C63%2C0%2C2&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-md="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/maureen-ohlhausen.jpg?id=29989904&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C63%2C0%2C2&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-sd="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/maureen-ohlhausen.jpg?id=29989904&amp;width=980&amp;quality=85" data-runner-style="padding-bottom: 56.25%;" data-type="image" role="img" style='background-image: url("https://www.protocol.com/media-library/maureen-ohlhausen.jpg?id=29989904&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C63%2C0%2C2&amp;height=700"); padding-bottom: 56.25%;'><div class="lazyload-placeholder">
<div class="runner-spinner">
<svg class="runner-spinner-circular" viewbox="25 25 50 50">
<circle class="runner-spinner-path" cx="50" cy="50" fill="none" r="20" stroke-miterlimit="10" stroke-width="3"></circle>
</svg>
</div>
</div></div>
</a>
</div><div class="widget__body clearfix sm-mt-1"><div class="photo-caption">

<a class="social-author__name" data-type="text" href="https://www.protocol.com/u/ben">Ben Brody</a>
<div class="social-author__bio"><p>
	Ben Brody (@
	<a href="https://twitter.com/BenBrodyDC" target="_blank">BenBrodyDC</a>) is a senior reporter at Protocol focusing on how Congress, courts and agencies affect the online world we live in. He formerly covered tech policy and lobbying (including antitrust, Section 230 and privacy) at Bloomberg News, where he previously reported on the influence industry, government ethics and the 2016 presidential election. Before that, Ben covered business news at CNNMoney and AdAge, and all manner of stories in and around New York. He still loves appearing on the New York news radio he grew up with.
</p></div>
</div><div class="social-date">
<span class="social-date__text">June 20, 2022</span>
</div><div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url css-listicle-body-2657525728" data-authors="Ben Brody" data-elid="2657525728" data-headline="The privacy debate could get 'dangerous' for the FTC" data-post-url="https://www.protocol.com/policy/ohlhausen-privacy-ftc"><div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief"><p>There’s one debate over privacy in Congress and another at the Federal Trade Commission, but the two are tightly linked, former FTC acting chair Maureen Ohlhausen told Protocol — and not in a way that’s good for the agency’s plans.</p><p>Ohlhausen, a Republican, is now co-chair of a group that represents internet service providers, and she testified at a House hearing Tuesday on the bipartisan, bicameral privacy <a href="https://www.protocol.com/bulletins/privacy-draft-2022" target="_self">proposal</a> currently under consideration. Although the measure still faces headwinds in the Senate, Ohlhausen said she thinks lawmakers are much closer to an agreement than when she left the FTC in 2018. She also said that, with some tweaks, the measure would have a lot more to offer the business sector than the status quo, despite the <a href="https://www.uschamber.com/technology/data-privacy/u-s-chamber-warns-it-will-oppose-any-privacy-legislation-that-creates-a-blanket-private-right-of-action#:~:text=A%20private%20right%20of%20action%20enables%20individuals%20to%20sue%20companies,plaintiffs'%20attorneys%20to%20generate%20cases." rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">complaints</a> from some powerful lobbying interests.</p></div>
<div class="content-container"></div>
<div class="js-appear-on-expand"><template><p>The conversation kept returning to the FTC, however, and for Ohlhausen, many of her concerns about where privacy rules could go revolve around the agency where she also spent more than a decade as a staffer. She sees danger in current chair Lina Khan’s push for the FTC to undertake a privacy <a href="https://www.protocol.com/newsletters/policy/khan-ftc-privacy-rules-congress" target="_self">rule-making</a>, as much to companies that use consumer information as to the agency itself. Khan has said she’s trying to reinvigorate an FTC that’s long let consumers down and deferred to big business, but Ohlhausen repeatedly joined Khan’s critics in urging her to stick to punishing companies one at a time, bring GOP commissioners back into decision-making and address staff discontent.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="1">
</div>
</div></p><p><em>This interview has been edited for length and clarity. </em></p><p><strong>You've testified in Congress about privacy legislation a few times over the last couple of years. How did <a href="https://energycommerce.house.gov/committee-activity/hearings/hearing-on-protecting-americas-consumers-bipartisan-legislation-to" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">this hearing</a> rate?</strong></p><p>I think it's enormous progress. We have a lot of different people representing different aspects of the ecosystem. They are generally supporting the bill. And we also saw a lot of bipartisan agreement, bicameral agreement. We can see progress from the first time I <a href="https://www.commerce.senate.gov/services/files/30994150-8879-48B7-9BC0-625D8C81A7F2" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">testified</a> on this after I left the FTC. You can see that there has been this movement of more people getting on board, more different pieces coming together. </p><p><strong>Preemption and consumer lawsuits have been the biggest fissures for years now, but lawmakers have mostly found compromises there. What did you hear at the hearing that seemed it might be among the toughest things for lawmakers to come together on now?</strong></p><p>You can see some of the issues involving the definitions. So [issues could arise on] the definition of “algorithm,” because that's very broad. I mean, the <a href="https://energycommerce.house.gov/sites/democrats.energycommerce.house.gov/files/documents/Bipartisan_Privacy_Discussion_Draft_Bill_Text.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">definition</a> is “a computational process including” — not<em> “limited to”</em> — “one derived from machine learning or artificial intelligence techniques, that makes or facilitates a decision or facilitates human decision making with respect to covered data.” That's really broad, because those other things don't limit it. It says it <em>includes</em> them. I think that's one of the concerns, that it can be extremely burdensome if you need to do one of these [algorithmic] impact assessments for every computational process you do on data that helps a human decision.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="2">
</div>
</div></p><p>And then the other [potential issue] was on sensitive covered data. Several members mentioned we need to preserve the business model that has allowed all these free, incredible services to be available to consumers. Targeted advertising seems to be explicitly allowed with opt-out, but some of the definitions of sensitive data make it unclear, because it seems like it would actually require opt-in. I think there was an understanding that you don't want to break a business model. You want to give additional protections there, and maybe you want to change it. But it has been a source of, I think, enormous consumer benefit and competitive benefits.</p><p><strong>A lot of the folks who are testifying along with you were suggesting fixes. But we’re also seeing the Chamber of Commerce more or less <a href="https://www.protocol.com/bulletins/privacy-draft-2022" target="_self">opposing</a> this. It kind of represents business writ large. Do you think the business community is going to try to stop it?</strong></p><p>I think the business community really wants this to pass. The position that the Chamber put forward, I wasn't hearing that from the Republican committee members. All the witnesses represented different constituencies, some of them other business constituencies. They were generally positive on it. They weren't saying, “Oh, rip this up and start over.” They were saying, “Well, you know, watch out here, clarify that, don't go too far here.” But no sense of general opposition. </p><p>There are concerns about some of the actions the FTC has been taking, and what it might do given very open-ended authority, but I've always said the way forward is for Congress to give the FTC clear direction. This bill really does that. FTC leadership wants to engage in very broad rule-making. But I think this bill is much more: Congress makes those hard cuts, [gives] clear guidance. The FTC has the enforcement role and the more limited role of filling in some of the details under congressional direction.</p><p>[The bill] needs some tweaking, but I think it really is a good path forward. I do think there is a lot of business support for it. I think they would rather have this bill than some sort of open-ended FTC rule-making. I think the expectation from some who want to push that [rule-making] is that they will somehow be able to put all these new restrictions in there. The FTC only has the authority Congress gave it. They shouldn’t be a legislature.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="3">
</div>
</div></p><p><strong>Chair Khan did a bunch of media interviews last week, and when she and I talked, she <a href="https://www.protocol.com/policy/khan-ftc-privacy-interview" target="_self">seemed</a> to be particularly interested in regulating data, maybe even if Congress goes ahead with this law.</strong></p><p>If this bill passes, I think it would be kind of odd for the FTC to go off on its own and do some separate rule-making. I think you've gotten pretty clear direction from Congress about where the boundaries are, where Congress has made the decision on balancing consumer rights and the beneficial uses of data. I would hope the FTC wouldn't go forward. They’re going to have enough to do under this bill.</p><p>The other thing is, when I was acting chairman for about a year and a half, we did plenty of privacy enforcement. It had to be bipartisan because it was just me and Terrell McSweeny: one Republican and one Democrat. Rule-making is not the only path. The FTC can proceed through case-by-case enforcement like it has, which I think has been fairly effective.</p><p>It's also possible that there might be some limited rule-making that Republicans would support, and you've seen that already in some other consumer protection areas. It's possible that Chair Khan wants to go down this road, but there are a lot of risks and opportunity costs. If you're doing [rule-making], you're not bringing enforcement cases. We've seen a <a href="https://www.marketwatch.com/story/biden-made-big-promises-on-antitrust-but-lina-khans-ftc-is-underachieving-critics-say-11654535910" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">big drop-off in enforcement</a> in her first year, and it's not because she didn't have a majority. I didn't have a majority, and enforcement did not fall off. Maybe she'll continue down that road, but I'm hopeful Congress passes this bill and then the FTC just moves forward on implementing the bill.</p><p><strong>People talk a lot about Khan’s tenure producing an unusually partisan commission. Do you think that's right? It's not as if there have never before been party-line votes, debates or stinging dissents.</strong></p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="4">
</div>
</div></p><p>I think this is very different. [During my time,] we got a lot done. There were a few things we disagreed on. We agreed to disagree, and we moved forward. And we kept up a high level of enforcement. Staff was very <a href="https://twitter.com/FTCPhillips/status/1521216285115359233" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">happy</a>, and that was at a time when there was a lot of anxiety: What's the Trump administration going to do? What we're seeing now is <a href="https://www.protocol.com/policy/khan-ftc-powers" target="_self">changes</a> to reduce the ability of other commissioners to get information to participate in things. I haven't seen that before. I was at the commission for six and a half years; I was in the majority for three months. But it was always very, very functional. I didn't feel, when I was in the minority in the Obama administration, that I was cut off. The chair’s office runs things, but it was on a much more collegial basis. And I think you see that reflected also in how the staff <a href="https://mlexmarketinsight.com/news-hub/editors-picks/area-of-expertise/antitrust/under-khans-leadership-staffers-air-frustrations-in-wake-of-survey" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">has</a> <a href="https://twitter.com/CSWilsonFTC/status/1519786949476761601" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">responded</a>. There's lots of reports about career staff <a href="https://nypost.com/2022/06/12/ftc-faces-staff-exodus-anger-over-biden-appointed-big-tech-foes-leadership/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">saying</a>, “The chair doesn't talk to us. We don't get an opportunity to weigh in.” I'm not in the building, but I think that reflects a different management style than we've ever seen at the FTC previously. </p><p><strong>I wonder if you could talk about some of the <a href="https://www.protocol.com/policy/khan-ftc-anniversary-privacy-kids" target="_self">external threats</a> that the FTC is facing. I'm thinking of the <a href="https://www.scotusblog.com/case-files/cases/axon-enterprise-inc-v-federal-trade-commission/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">Axon</a> <a href="https://www.protocol.com/newsletters/policy/amazon-google-apple-anti-antitrust?rebelltitem=1" target="_self">case</a> coming up to the Supreme Court. It seems like, by next year, the justices may well say that companies facing the FTC’s internal, court-like system can challenge the whole process in federal court and make the FTC go through a sort of <a href="https://www.protocol.com/newsletters/policy/sec-ftc-losses?rebelltitem=10#rebelltitem10" target="_self">pre-litigation litigation</a>.</strong></p><p>I used to say, “Avoid doing things that unite your enemies.” The FTC is a small agency with a vague statute. When it stuck generally to case-by-case enforcement on the antitrust side, and seeking redress in limited cases against fraudsters, it got a lot of support. Congress was happy. We brought cases that big companies didn't like, not saying that we didn’t. We challenged things, but the current FTC is pushing into saying, “Well, we now want to take on much more of a regulatory role. We want to do rule-making.” It's at a time also where the fundamental structure of agencies like the FTC is being re-examined. The FTC is facing a lot of headwinds in that area, and it needs to be careful. People say, “The FTC can do this, and they can do that, and all they risk is losing that case.” And I say that's absolutely not true. They gambled on disgorgement and <a href="https://www.protocol.com/ftc-13b-congress" target="_self">lost</a> <a href="https://www.supremecourt.gov/opinions/20pdf/19-508_l6gn.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank">big</a> [at the Supreme Court], because it brought together enough people saying, “There aren't enough guardrails on this. It's not rooted in the statute.” That's kind of what I see here: concern about pushing against all these boundaries that will put the entire structure of the agency at risk. Pushing these aggressive theories at a time when the agency’s foundational structure is being questioned just creates a dangerous possibility that the agency loses more than just that case.</p><p><div class="ad-tag"><div class="ad-place-holder" data-pos="5">
</div>
</div></p></template></div>

</div></div>

He formerly covered tech policy and lobbying (including antitrust, Section 230 and privacy) at Bloomberg News, where he previously reported on the influence industry, government ethics and the 2016 presidential election. Before that, Ben covered business news at CNNMoney and AdAge, and all manner of stories in and around New York. He still loves appearing on the New York news radio he grew up with.

<div class="widget tag-electrification tag-heat-pumps tag-hvac post-section--climate" data-category="Climate" elid="2657527014"><article class="clearfix page-article sm-mb-1 quality-HD post-2657527014" data-is-frozen="false" elid="2657527014">
<div class="row px10"><div class="col sm-mb-1" id="col-center" style="width:100.0%;"><div class="widget__body clearfix sm-mt-1">
<div class="post-tags-primary">
<a class="post-tags-primary-link" href="/tag/hvac">hvac</a>
</div>
<a class="widget__section js-load-brick-on-click" href="https://www.protocol.com/climate/">Climate</a>
<h2 class="widget__headline h2">
<a aria-label="There’s never been a better time for a heat pump revolution" class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" data-type="text" href="https://www.protocol.com/climate/heat-pump-hvac-climate-change">
        It’s time to get serious about heat pumps
    </a>
</h2>
<div class="widget__subheadline">
<h2 class="widget__subheadline-text h2" data-type="text"><p>Heat pumps are quickly emerging as the HVAC technology of the future. We just need more of them.</p></h2>
</div></div><div class="widget__head"><a aria-label="There’s never been a better time for a heat pump revolution" href="https://www.protocol.com/climate/heat-pump-hvac-climate-change"><div aria-label="An aerial view of houses packed close together. " class="widget__image crop-16x9" data-runner-img-hd="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/an-aerial-view-of-houses-packed-close-together.jpg?id=29781746&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C312%2C0%2C312&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-md="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/an-aerial-view-of-houses-packed-close-together.jpg?id=29781746&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C312%2C0%2C312&amp;height=700" data-runner-img-sd="https://www.protocol.com/media-library/an-aerial-view-of-houses-packed-close-together.jpg?id=29781746&amp;width=980&amp;quality=85" data-runner-style="padding-bottom: 56.25%;" data-type="image" role="img" style='background-image: url("https://www.protocol.com/media-library/an-aerial-view-of-houses-packed-close-together.jpg?id=29781746&amp;width=1245&amp;quality=85&amp;coordinates=0%2C312%2C0%2C312&amp;height=700"); padding-bottom: 56.25%;'><div class="lazyload-placeholder">
<div class="runner-spinner">
<svg class="runner-spinner-circular" viewbox="25 25 50 50">
<circle class="runner-spinner-path" cx="50" cy="50" fill="none" r="20" stroke-miterlimit="10" stroke-width="3"></circle>
</svg>
</div>
</div></div>
</a>
</div><div class="widget__body clearfix sm-mt-1"><div class="photo-caption">
<p>It's time for heat pumps.</p>
</div><div class="photo-credit">
        Photo: Breno Assis/Unsplash

<span class="social-date-modified__text">June 19, 2022</span>
